2:24:1118:0:14:72:427:700:12:Times:12:Times:Effects of the Affordable Care Act on Health Behaviors after Three Years
2:25:1190:14:14:72:521:686:12:Times:12:Times:Charles Courtemanche, James Marton, Benjamin Ukert, Aaron Yelowitz, and Daniela Zapata
2:26:1276:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:NBER Working Paper No. 24511
2:27:1304:14:14:72:124:658:12:Times:12:Times:April 2018
2:28:1314:14:28:72:169:644:12:Times:12:Times:JEL No. I12,I13,I18
2:29:1333:28:28:273:338:616:12:Times:12:Times:ABSTRACT
2:30:1341:28:14:72:546:588:12:Times:12:Times:This  paper  examines  the  impacts  of  the  Affordable  Care  Act  (ACA)  –  which  substantially 
2:31:1441:14:14:72:545:574:12:Times:12:Times:increased insurance coverage through regulations, mandates, subsidies, and Medicaid expansions 
2:32:1535:14:14:72:545:560:12:Times:12:Times:– on behaviors related to future health risks after three years. Using data from the Behavioral Risk 
2:33:1635:14:14:72:545:546:12:Times:12:Times:Factor  Surveillance System  and  an  identification  strategy  that  leverages  variation  in  pre-ACA 
2:34:1738:14:14:72:546:532:12:Times:12:Times:uninsured  rates  and  state Medicaid  expansion  decisions,  we  show  that  the  ACA  increased 
2:35:1835:14:14:72:545:518:12:Times:12:Times:preventive  care  utilization  along  several dimensions,  but  also  increased  risky  drinking.  These 
2:36:1939:14:14:72:545:504:12:Times:12:Times:results are driven by  the private portions of  the law,  as  opposed  to the Medicaid expansion. We 
2:37:2039:14:28:72:326:490:12:Times:12:Times:also conduct subsample analyses by income and age.
2:38:2089:28:14:72:182:462:12:Times:12:Times:Charles Courtemanche
2:39:2109:14:14:72:191:448:12:Times:12:Times:Georgia State University
2:40:2133:14:14:72:268:434:12:Times:12:Times:Andrew Young School of Policy Studies

3:72:2813:0:27:72:140:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:Introduction 
3:73:2826:27:28:108:531:681:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f4: Emerging literatures in health economics and health policy seek to evaluate the impacts 
3:74:2914:28:27:71:540:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of the primary components of the Affordable Care Act (ACA), including the individual mandate, 
3:75:3008:27:28:71:515:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:subsidized Marketplace coverage, and state Medicaid expansions, that were implemented in 
3:76:3097:28:28:71:535:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:2014 (Obama 2014). While the stated goals of the ACA include improving access to health care 
3:77:3190:28:27:71:505:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in order to improve health outcomes, economists have long understood that expansions of 
3:78:3278:27:28:71:519:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:insurance coverage could plausibly influence investments in health capital in either direction 
3:79:3373:28:27:71:528:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:(e.g. Cawley and Ruhm 2011; Barbaresco et al. 2015). The purpose of this paper is to estimate 
3:80:3467:27:28:71:537:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the impacts of the ACA on behaviors related to future health risks. These include both behaviors 
3:81:3564:28:28:71:502:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that reduce future risks (such as utilization of preventive medical services) and those that 
3:82:3657:28:27:71:523:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:increase risks (such as smoking and drinking). Our primary innovation is to use data from the 
3:83:3751:27:28:71:533:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:2011-2016 Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System (BRFSS), which gives us a longer post-
3:84:3842:28:27:71:467:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:treatment period than prior studies and therefore a greater ability to detect effects. 
3:85:3929:27:28:107:537:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The net effects of health insurance coverage on both preventive care utilization and risky 
3:86:4020:28:27:71:531:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:health behaviors depend on the relative roles of out-of-pocket prices, ex ante moral hazard, and 
3:87:4117:27:28:71:530:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:income effects. By lowering the portion of medical costs borne by the patient, health insurance 
3:88:4213:28:28:71:521:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reduces the price of preventive care as well as medications and counseling services related to 
3:89:4308:28:27:71:503:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:risky behaviors. The direct price effect should therefore work in the direction of healthier 
3:90:4401:27:28:71:533:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:lifestyles along both dimensions. However, expansions in insurance coverage could also lead to 
3:91:4496:28:27:71:530:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:less healthy lifestyles through ex ante moral hazard, the phenomenon in which the reduction in 
3:92:4591:27:28:71:512:157:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:financial risk associated with unhealthy behavior incentivizes such behavior. While ex ante 
3:93:4682:28:28:71:524:129:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:moral hazard has most often been examined in the context of risky health behaviors, the same 
3:94:4775:28:0:71:536:101:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:logic could also apply to a failure to adhere to screening and vaccination guidelines (Barbaresco 
4:95:4876:0:27:72:508:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:et al. 2015; Simon et al. 2017). In addition, income effects from gaining free or subsidized 
4:96:4969:27:28:72:526:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:coverage could influence behaviors in potentially conflicting ways. Consumers may choose to 
4:97:5061:28:27:72:525:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:spend money they had budgeted for the direct purchase of medical care on alcohol, cigarettes, 
4:98:5155:27:28:72:504:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and junk food or, conversely, on healthy food and gym memberships (Simon et al. 2017). 
4:99:5241:28:28:108:524:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Following Courtemanche et al. (2017, 2018a), we estimate difference-in-difference-in-
4:100:5326:28:27:72:535:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:differences (DDD) models to evaluate the impact of the ACA, where the differences come from 
4:101:5418:27:28:72:523:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:time, state Medicaid expansion status, and local area pretreatment uninsured rate. Most of the 
4:102:5513:28:27:72:529:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:previous ACA literature tends to focus on the effects of the ACA Medicaid expansions using a 
4:103:5606:27:28:72:515:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:simpler difference-in-differences (DD) model that compares changes over time in Medicaid 
4:104:5695:28:28:72:534:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expansion states to changes in non-expansion states. We seek to identify the impact of the other 
4:105:5792:28:27:72:519:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:components of the ACA as well, such as the individual mandate and subsidized Marketplace 
4:106:5881:27:28:72:533:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:coverage.1 This is inherently more challenging because these components were implemented in 
4:107:5973:28:27:72:532:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:every state simultaneously. Our third difference addresses this issue by exploiting an additional 
4:108:6071:27:28:72:531:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:layer of plausibly exogenous variation arising from the fact that the national components of the 
4:109:6168:28:27:72:516:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ACA should provide the most intense “treatment” in local areas with the highest pre-reform 
4:110:6259:27:28:72:154:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:uninsured rates.  
4:111:6277:28:28:108:539:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The BRFSS is well-suited for our study because it includes a number of questions related 
4:112:6366:28:27:72:520:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to health behaviors and other outcomes of interest. In addition, it is large enough to precisely 
4:113:6463:27:28:72:506:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:estimate the effects of state policies, with over 300,000 observations per year. Our sample 
4:114:6555:28:27:72:449:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:consists of nonelderly adults included in the 2011–2016 waves of the BRFSS. 
4:115:6631:27:28:108:526:157:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Our results provide some evidence of both improved access to preventive services and 
4:116:6716:28:32:72:500:129:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the presence of ex ante moral hazard. For the full sample of non-elderly adults, the ACA 
4:117:6805:32:7:72:218:97:121:g_d0_f2:121:g_d0_f2:                                                            
4:118:6865:7:16:72:511:90:42:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:1 Our results could also partially reflect the ACA’s employer mandate, which took effect in 2015 and became 
4:119:6973:16:0:71:403:74:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:stronger in 2016. See https://obamacarefacts.com/obamacare-employer-mandate/.  
5:120:7055:0:27:72:515:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:increased several aspects of preventive care utilization (well-patient checkups, pap and HIV 
5:121:7148:27:28:72:516:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:tests, and mammograms) but also risky drinking in non-Medicaid-expansion states, with the 
5:122:7238:28:27:72:499:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:effects being statistically indistinguishable in Medicaid expansion states. No statistically 
5:123:7331:27:28:72:530:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:significant results emerge for flu shots, body mass index (BMI), drinks per month, smoking, or 
5:124:7426:28:28:72:520:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:exercising. Event study regressions provide support for our econometric approach while also 
5:125:7518:28:27:72:533:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:revealing some evidence of additional ex ante moral hazard – greater smoking and less exercise 
5:126:7613:27:28:72:541:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:– in the ACA’s third year. Finally, we conduct subsample analyses by income and age. The gains 
5:127:7708:28:27:72:515:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in preventive care are largely concentrated among the lower half of the income distribution, 
5:128:7801:27:28:72:541:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:while the results suggesting ex ante moral hazard are clearer among the upper half. The increases 
5:129:7899:28:28:72:534:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in pap and HIV tests are concentrated among those below the sample median age, while the rise 
5:130:7993:28:27:72:357:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in mammograms is driven by those above the median age.  
5:131:8049:27:28:108:531:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:These findings with three years of post-reform data build on analyses by Courtemanche 
5:132:8135:28:27:72:492:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:et al. (2018a) and Simon et al. (2017) that also used the BRFSS but only through 2015. 
5:133:8222:27:28:72:522:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Moreover, Courtemanche et al. (2018a) only examine a single preventive care outcome (well-
5:134:8312:28:27:72:539:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:patient checkups), while Simon et al. (2017) only estimate causal effects of the ACA’s Medicaid 
5:135:8408:27:23:72:127:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expansion. 
5:136:8419:23:19:72:74:272:36:g_d0_f3:36:g_d0_f3: 
5:137:8419:19:27:72:168:253:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f4:Literature Review 
5:138:8436:27:28:108:533:226:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The RAND Health Insurance Experiment of the 1970s–1980s provides some of the first 
5:139:8519:28:28:72:537:198:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:causally interpretable evidence on the impacts of health insurance coverage on health behaviors. 
5:140:8616:28:27:72:523:170:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Randomized variation in the generosity of insurance coverage through the experiment did not 
5:141:8708:27:28:72:538:143:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:lead to statistically significant changes in smoking behavior or weight (Brook et al. 1983). There 
5:142:8807:28:27:72:521:115:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:is also a large literature focusing on Medicaid expansions prior to the ACA. In terms of risky 
5:143:8902:27:0:72:538:88:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:health behaviors, Medicaid expansions for children and pregnant women in the 1980s and 1990s 
6:144:8998:0:27:72:518:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reduced low birthweight (Currie and Gruber 1996a), but increased smoking among pregnant 
6:145:9086:27:28:72:289:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:women (Dave, Kaestner, and Wehby 2015).  
6:146:9127:28:27:108:497:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Studies of the more recent randomized 2008 Oregon Medicaid lottery found that 
6:147:9205:27:28:72:533:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Medicaid coverage increased use of preventive services but did not have statistically significant 
6:148:9303:28:28:72:495:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:effects on smoking or obesity (Finkelstein et al. 2012; Baicker et al. 2013). Kolstad and 
6:149:9393:28:27:72:524:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Kowalski (2012) found that the expansion of coverage resulting from the 2006 Massachusetts 
6:150:9484:27:28:72:542:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:insurance reform increased utilization of some types of preventive care, while Courtemanche and 
6:151:9580:28:27:72:522:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Zapata (2014) found that it reduced BMI but did not significantly influence smoking. Among 
6:152:9671:27:28:72:535:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:seniors, Dave and Kaestner (2009) documented slightly worsening smoking and drinking habits 
6:153:9763:28:28:72:504:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:as a result of gaining Medicare coverage, while Card et al. (2008) found mixed results for 
6:154:9854:28:27:72:152:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:preventive care. 
6:155:9871:27:28:108:537:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:In terms of the ACA, one strand of the literature examines the 2010 mandate for insurers 
6:156:9960:28:27:72:494:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to cover dependents up to 26 years old. Evidence suggests that this dependent coverage 
6:157:10047:27:28:72:534:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expansion did not impact the utilization of preventive services, but did reduce BMI (Barbaresco 
6:158:10143:28:27:72:530:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:et al. 2015). Another strand of literature examines the major components of the ACA that were 
6:159:10237:27:28:72:524:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:implemented in 2014. Simon et al. (2017) used the BRFSS and found that the ACA Medicaid 
6:160:10325:28:28:72:521:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expansion increased some aspects of preventive care use among low income childless adults. 
6:161:10416:28:27:72:496:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:However, they did not find any evidence of effects on risky health behaviors. Similarly, 
6:162:10505:27:28:72:515:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Courtemanche et al. (2018a) showed that the full ACA increased well-patient checkups, but 
6:163:10595:28:27:72:519:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:found no evidence of effects on risky health behaviors. Cotti et al. (2017) use scanner, rather 
6:164:10691:27:28:72:499:157:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:than survey, data and find little evidence that the ACA’s Medicaid expansion influenced 
6:165:10779:28:0:72:333:129:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:purchases of snack foods, soda, cigarettes, or alcohol. 
7:166:10838:0:27:108:496:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Relative to the previous literature, we make two main contributions. First, to our 
7:167:10921:27:28:72:520:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:knowledge, we provide the first causally interpretable evidence on the effects of the ACA on 
7:168:11014:28:27:72:499:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:health behaviors after three years. Simon, Soni, and Cawley (2017), Courtemanche et al. 
7:169:11102:27:28:72:530:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:(2018a), and Cotti et al. (2017) only used two years of post-treatment data. Since none of these 
7:170:11199:28:28:72:502:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:studies found effects on risky behaviors, the addition of the third post-treatment year will 
7:171:11292:28:27:72:542:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ultimately prove essential for documenting any ex ante moral hazard. The importance of the third 
7:172:11389:27:28:72:529:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:year is not surprising. Enrollment in the Marketplaces has continued to rise each year since the 
7:173:11486:28:27:72:540:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ACA took effect, and the estimated gain in insurance coverage from the fully implemented ACA 
7:174:11579:27:28:72:513:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:rose from 6.5 percentage points in 2014 to 9.7 percentage points in 2015 to 11.8 percentage 
7:175:11671:28:28:72:536:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:points in 2016 (Courtemanche et al., 2018b). Moreover, a long literature on economic models of 
7:176:11766:28:27:72:521:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:addiction suggests that health habits – particularly those related to addictive substances – can 
7:177:11863:27:28:72:471:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:take time to adjust in response to changing incentives (Cawley and Ruhm, 2011).   
7:178:11945:28:27:108:523:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Second, we also conduct, to our knowledge, the first investigation of the effects of the 
7:179:12034:27:28:72:502:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:fully implemented ACA (including the provisions related to private coverage) on a broad 
7:180:12122:28:27:72:506:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:spectrum of preventive care measures. Simon, Soni, and Cawley (2017) examined several 
7:181:12208:27:28:72:538:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:preventive care outcomes but only offered causally interpretable results for the ACA’s Medicaid 
7:182:12304:28:28:72:533:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expansion. Courtemanche et al. (2018a) estimated the impacts of both the private and Medicaid 
7:183:12398:28:27:72:513:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expansion components of the ACA, but only considered one preventive care measure (well-
7:184:12485:27:22:72:165:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:patient checkups).  
7:185:12505:22:20:72:74:190:36:g_d0_f3:36:g_d0_f3: 
7:186:12505:20:27:72:100:170:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:Data 
7:187:12510:27:28:108:532:143:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:We use data from the BRFSS, an annual telephone survey conducted by the US Centers 
7:188:12593:28:27:72:520:115:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in conjunction with state health departments. The 
7:189:12684:27:0:72:541:88:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:BRFSS collects information on various types of health behaviors for all 50 states and the District 
8:190:12786:0:27:72:533:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of Columbia. Having a large sample size (more than 300,000 adults per year) is critical because 
8:191:12882:27:28:72:496:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the ACA affected insurance coverage for only a fraction of the population, thus limiting 
8:192:12971:28:27:71:180:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:plausible effect sizes.  
8:193:12996:27:28:107:507:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Our sample consists of BRFSS respondents 19-64 years old who were interviewed 
8:194:13074:28:28:71:535:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:between 2011 and 2016. Older individuals were excluded because the ACA was not intended to 
8:195:13165:28:27:71:539:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:affect their health care coverage. We start our sample with calendar year 2011 because this is the 
8:196:13264:27:28:71:529:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:first year in which the BRFSS included cell phones in its sampling frame. Thus our 2011-2016 
8:197:13357:28:27:71:525:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sample period gives us a total of three years of pre-reform data and three years of post-reform 
8:198:13453:27:28:71:97:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:data. 
8:199:13459:28:28:107:526:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:We examine ten dependent variables related to preventive care or risky behaviors. Our 
8:200:13545:28:27:71:517:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:preventive care outcomes are indicators for a recent well-patient checkup (e.g. physical), flu 
8:201:13640:27:28:71:514:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:shot, pap test, and mammogram in the past year, and whether the person was ever tested for 
8:202:13731:28:27:71:542:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:HIV.2 Our risky health behavior outcomes are a binary indicator for whether one smokes, a count 
8:203:13827:27:28:72:539:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of alcoholic drinks consumed in the past month, an indicator for whether one was a risky drinker 
8:204:13924:28:27:72:535:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in the last month,3 a continuous variable measuring the respondents’ body weight in the form of 
8:205:14020:27:36:72:533:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:BMI,4 and a binary variable for any exercise in the last month.5 Smoking, drinking, and obesity-
8:206:14116:36:7:72:218:259:121:g_d0_f2:121:g_d0_f2:                                                            
8:207:14176:7:17:72:538:252:42:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f4:2 The BRFSS survey questions we use to build the preventive care outcomes are: “About how long has it been since 
8:208:14288:17:12:71:526:235:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:you last visited a doctor for a routine checkup?” A routine checkup is a general physical exam, not an exam for a 
8:209:14402:12:11:72:535:223:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:specific injury, illness, or condition; during the past 12 months, “have you had either a flu shot or a flu vaccine that 
8:210:14523:11:12:72:529:212:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:was sprayed in your nose?”; “how long has it been since you had your last Pap test?”; “how long has it been since 
8:211:14637:12:11:72:524:200:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:you had your last mammogram?”; and “not counting tests you may have had as part of blood donation, have you 
8:212:14745:11:7:72:338:189:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f2:ever been tested for HIV? Include testing fluid from your mouth”. 
8:213:14811:7:16:72:521:182:42:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:3 According to the CDC (https://www.cdc.gov/alcohol/fact-sheets/alcohol-use.htm), risky drinking is defined as 
8:214:14922:16:12:72:520:166:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:consuming 60 or more (32 or more) drinks in the last month for men (women), or having any episodes of binge 
8:215:15030:12:11:72:538:154:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:drinking in the last month. Binge drinking is defined as having five or more drinks in one occasion for men and four 
8:216:15147:11:12:72:540:143:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:or more drinks in one occasion for women. We also considered using binge drinking as an outcome; the results were 
8:217:15261:12:7:72:232:131:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:very similar to those for risky drinking. 
8:218:15303:7:16:72:535:124:42:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:4 BMI is defined as weight in kilograms divided by height in squared meters. We also considered a binary outcome 
8:219:15416:16:11:72:541:108:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:for obesity (BMI ≥ 30) and the results were qualitatively similar to those for BMI. The BRFSS computes BMI based 
8:220:15529:11:12:72:512:97:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:on respondents’ reported height and weight. Self-reported height and weight are widely known to suffer from 
8:221:15637:12:11:72:523:85:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:measurement error, but attempting to correct for this error rarely has a meaningful effect on regression estimates 
8:222:15752:11:0:72:190:74:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f2:(Courtemanche et al., 2015).  
9:223:15785:0:27:72:538:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:related behaviors were selected because they represent three of the leading causes of preventable 
9:224:15883:27:28:72:540:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:death in the United States, costing 467,000, 64,000, and 216,000 lives respectively per year as of 
9:225:15982:28:27:72:325:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:2005 (Danaei et al. 2009; Cawley and Ruhm 2012).  
9:226:16032:27:28:108:540:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Our analysis includes controls for demographic characteristics, household characteristics, 
9:227:16123:28:28:72:526:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:economic characteristics, and measures that capture state differences in the implementation of 
9:228:16218:28:27:72:540:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the ACA. Specifically, we use BRFSS information to construct dummy variables for age groups, 
9:229:16311:27:28:72:523:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:gender, race/ethnicity, marital status, education, household income, number of children in the 
9:230:16406:28:27:72:537:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:household, whether the respondent reports their primary occupation to be a student, and whether 
9:231:16502:27:28:72:532:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the respondent is unemployed. Additionally, we use data from the Bureau of Labor Statistics to 
9:232:16597:28:28:72:479:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:control for seasonally adjusted monthly state unemployment rate. Finally, following 
9:233:16681:28:27:72:530:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Courtemanche et al. (2017, 2018a), we include dummy variables for whether states set up their 
9:234:16775:27:28:72:501:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:own insurance exchanges and whether these exchanges experienced glitches (KFF 2014; 
9:235:16859:28:27:72:155:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Kowalski 2014). 
9:236:16875:27:28:108:501:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:One of our treatment variables, measuring the “dose” of the ACA’s impact, is the 
9:237:16956:28:27:72:533:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:uninsured rate in the respondent’s “local area” in the pre-reform year of 2013, computed within 
9:238:17052:27:28:72:542:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:our BRFSS sample. Defining local areas is not straightforward since the BRFSS does not contain 
9:239:17147:28:28:72:521:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:narrow geographic identifiers such as county. Beyond state of residence, the only geographic 
9:240:17240:28:27:72:537:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:information available in the BRFSS is an “area type” variable indicating whether the respondent 
9:241:17336:27:33:72:518:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:resides in the center city of an MSA, outside the center city of an MSA but inside the county 
9:242:17430:33:8:72:542:179:121:g_d0_f2:121:g_d0_f2:                                                                                                                                                                                                
9:243:17622:8:17:72:539:171:42:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f4:5 For the risky health behaviors we used the following questions and pre-calculated variables in the BFRSS; “do you 
9:244:17738:17:11:72:515:154:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:now smoke cigarettes every day, some days, or not at all?”; the calculated total number of alcoholic beverages 
9:245:17849:11:12:72:534:143:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:consumed per month, the calculated BMI based on questions regarding height and weight, “During the past month, 
9:246:17960:12:11:72:532:131:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:other than your regular job, did you participate in any physical activities or exercises such as running, calisthenics, 
9:247:18080:11:12:72:537:120:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:golf, gardening, or walking for exercise?”. We created the risky drinker variable ourselves based on the government 
9:248:18196:12:11:72:506:108:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:definition in footnote 2, the calculated alcohol consumption variable mentioned above, and a binge drinking 
9:249:18304:11:12:72:536:97:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:question that states, “Considering all types of alcoholic beverages, how many times during the past 30 days did you 
9:250:18420:12:11:72:375:85:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:have X [X = 5 for men, X = 4 for women] or more drinks on an occasion?” 
9:251:18492:11:0:72:75:74:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4: 
10:252:18495:0:27:72:527:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:containing the center city, inside a suburban county of the MSA, or not in an MSA. Moreover, 
10:253:18588:27:28:72:525:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the “area type” variable is missing for cell-phone respondents. Following Courtemanche et al. 
10:254:18682:28:27:72:506:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:(2018a), we define four “local areas” within each state – those living within a central city, 
10:255:18776:27:28:72:500:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:suburbs, non-MSA, and location unavailable (i.e., cell phone sample) – and calculate the 
10:256:18865:28:28:72:536:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:pretreatment average uninsured rates accordingly. This approach produces a total of 194 “areas” 
10:257:18961:28:27:72:528:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:(some states do not have all four area types) with 2013 uninsured rates that are computed from 
10:258:19056:27:28:72:506:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:between 219 to 5,804 respondents each, with the average being 1,475 respondents and the 
10:259:19144:28:27:72:240:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:median being 1,205 respondents.6  
10:260:19178:27:28:108:524:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Our Medicaid expansion variable is constructed based on information collected by the 
10:261:19263:28:28:72:535:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Kaiser Family Foundation (KFF). According to the KFF, 32 states expanded Medicaid by 2016. 
10:262:19354:28:27:72:509:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Most states expanded Medicaid in January 2014, with a handful of exceptions. Michigan’s 
10:263:19442:27:28:72:522:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expansion (April) and New Hampshire’s expansion (August) both took place during 2014. In 
10:264:19531:28:27:72:505:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:addition, Pennsylvania, Indiana and Alaska expanded Medicaid in January, February, and 
10:265:19618:27:28:72:539:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:September of 2015, respectively. Finally, Montana and Louisiana expanded Medicaid in January 
10:266:19711:28:27:72:525:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and July of 2016, respectively. We classify states as part of the Medicaid expansion treatment 
10:267:19806:27:28:72:302:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:group beginning the month of their expansions. 
10:268:19853:28:28:108:538:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Table 1 provides pretreatment means and standard deviations for our preventive care and 
10:269:19941:28:27:71:508:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:risky health behavior dependent variables, stratified into four groups based on whether the 
10:270:20033:27:28:71:532:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:respondent’s state expanded Medicaid and whether her local area’s pretreatment uninsured rate 
10:271:20127:28:29:71:528:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:was above or below the median within the sample.7 Table A1 in the Online Appendix does the 
10:272:20218:29:8:72:218:155:121:g_d0_f2:121:g_d0_f2:                                                            
10:273:20278:8:16:72:512:147:42:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:6 The lack of county identifiers precludes other possible measures of the “dose” of the ACA’s impact, such as 
10:274:20388:16:7:72:461:131:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:county-level variation in physician density, that would have to be computed outside the BRFSS.  
10:275:20484:7:16:72:521:124:42:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:7 We were able to compare the mean baseline BRFSS values of the majority of our health behavior measures of 
10:276:20592:16:13:71:520:108:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:interest with publically reported values from the CDC (NCHS, 2017) as a check on their validity. Our measures 
10:277:20702:13:12:71:528:95:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:were generally consistent with those compiled by the CDC which gives us confident that our results are not being 
10:278:20815:12:0:71:346:83:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:driven by data collection idiosyncrasies associated with the BRFSS. 
11:279:20887:0:27:72:509:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:same for our control variables. According to Table 1, 63 percent of respondents had a well 
11:280:20978:27:28:72:531:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:patient checkup in the past year, 32 percent had a flu shot, 56 percent had a pap test, 36 percent 
11:281:21077:28:27:72:522:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:had a mammogram, and 44 percent have ever had an HIV test. All preventive care outcomes, 
11:282:21166:27:28:72:527:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:with the exception of having a HIV test, had higher pretreatment rates in Medicaid expansions 
11:283:21260:28:28:72:510:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:states, especially in states with below the median baseline uninsured rate. Our econometric 
11:284:21352:28:27:72:324:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:strategy will account for these baseline differences.  
11:285:21407:27:28:108:520:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:In terms of risky health behaviors, the average BMI of the sample in the pretreatment 
11:286:21493:28:27:71:526:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:period was 27.9, 22 percent were smokers, the sample average was almost 15 alcoholic drinks 
11:287:21585:27:28:71:513:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:per month, and about 21 percent of the sample was engaged in risky drinking. Pretreatment 
11:288:21675:28:28:72:537:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:smoking and binge drinking rates and the number of drinks per month were higher in states with 
11:289:21770:28:27:72:536:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:baseline uninsured rates above the median, while BMI was higher among non-expansion states.  
11:290:21863:27:28:107:534:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Figure 1 shows how the average values of the preventive care outcome variables change 
11:291:21949:28:27:71:531:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:across the sample period for four groups stratified by state Medicaid expansion status and local 
11:292:22046:27:28:71:535:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:area pretreatment uninsured rate (above or below the median). Well-patient doctor visits and flu 
11:293:22143:28:27:71:505:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:shots were trending upward before the ACA’s 2014 implementation, while Pap tests were 
11:294:22229:27:28:71:499:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:declining and mammograms and HIV tests were relatively flat. Importantly, for all these 
11:295:22317:28:28:71:515:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:outcomes the pretreatment trends appear relatively similar across the four groups, providing 
11:296:22410:28:27:71:514:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:preliminary support for an identification strategy based on pretreatment uninsured rates and 
11:297:22503:27:28:71:535:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Medicaid expansion decisions. After the ACA took effect, we see some evidence of increases in 
11:298:22597:28:27:71:495:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:some of these measures – most notably pap and HIV tests in 2015 – but an econometric 
11:299:22682:27:28:71:464:157:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:evaluation is necessary to determine whether these are causal effects of the law.   
11:300:22766:28:28:107:517:129:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Figure 2 presents similar graphs for the risky behavior outcomes. Prior to the ACA’s 
11:301:22851:28:14:72:490:101:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:implementation, BMI was trending upward while smoking and drinking were trending 
11:302:22932:14:13:72:542:87:121:g_d0_f2:121:g_d0_f2:                                                                                                                                                                                                
11:303:23124:13:0:72:74:74:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2: 
12:304:23129:0:27:72:523:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:downward and exercise did not exhibit a clear pattern. Again, pretreatment trends are broadly 
12:305:23223:27:28:72:540:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:similar across the four groups. After the ACA, BMI and smoking appear to largely continue their 
12:306:23319:28:27:72:535:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:pre-existing trends, while the downward trend in drinking disappears and actually turns positive 
12:307:23416:27:22:72:156:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:for some groups. 
12:308:23433:22:20:108:110:604:36:g_d0_f4:36:g_d0_f4: 
12:309:23433:20:27:72:120:584:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:Methods 
12:310:23441:27:28:108:504:557:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:We seek to estimate the effects of both the fully implemented ACA (including the 
12:311:23522:28:28:72:541:529:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Medicaid expansion) and the ACA without the Medicaid expansion for each outcome. The major 
12:312:23613:28:27:72:501:501:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:challenge associated with this objective is in disentangling the impacts of the nationwide 
12:313:23704:27:28:71:520:474:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:components of the ACA from underlying annual fluctuations in our outcomes of interest that 
12:314:23795:28:27:72:531:446:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:would have occurred even in the absence of the ACA. In this paper we adopt the DDD strategy 
12:315:23887:27:28:72:532:419:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Courtemanche et al. (2017) use to identify the impact of the ACA on health insurance coverage 
12:316:23981:28:28:72:522:391:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:after one year and that Courtemanche et al. (2018a) use to estimate the effect on access, risky 
12:317:24077:28:27:72:526:363:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:health behaviors, and self-assessed health after two years. Such an approach exploits sub-state 
12:318:24173:27:28:72:533:336:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:geographic variation in the intensity of treatment arising from differential pre-reform uninsured 
12:319:24271:28:27:72:540:308:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:rates. Adding this layer of sub-state variation allows us to include time period fixed effects while 
12:320:24372:27:28:72:396:281:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:still identifying the effects of the national components of the ACA. 
12:321:24441:28:27:108:511:253:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Assuming that a geographic area’s treatment intensity is proportional to its baseline 
12:322:24527:27:28:72:382:226:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:(2013) uninsured rate, our DDD model is given by equation (1): 
12:323:24590:28:29:124:490:198:144:g_d0_f6:144:g_d0_f4:ݕ௜௔௦௧ߛൌ଴ߛ൅ଵܦܧܴܷܵܰܫܷܰሺ௔௦ܱܶܵܲ∗௧ߛሻ൅ଶܦܫܣܥܫܦܧܯሺ௦௧ܱܶܵܲ∗௧ሻ൅ 
12:324:24642:29:28:118:543:169:144:g_d0_f6:144:g_d0_f4:ߛଷܦܧܴܷܵܰܫܷܰሺ௔௦ܦܫܣܥܫܦܧܯ∗௦௧ܱܶܵܲ∗௧ߛሻ൅ସࢄ࢚࢙ࢇ࢏ߠ൅௔௧ߙ൅௔௦ߝ൅௜௔௦௧															(1) 
12:325:24715:28:28:72:540:141:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:where ݕ௜௔௦௧ is a generic health behavior outcome described in table 1 for individual i in area type 
12:326:24815:28:28:72:511:113:144:Times:144:g_d0_f4:a in state s in time period t, ܱܶܵܲ௧ is an indicator for whether period t is in the post-reform

13:328:25011:0:28:72:516:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:table 2, ܦܫܣܥܫܦܧܯ௦௧ is an indicator for whether state s participated in the ACA’s Medicaid 
13:329:25102:28:28:71:540:680:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expansion as of time (month/year) t, and ܦܧܴܷܵܰܫܷܰ௔௦ is the 2013 (pre-reform) uninsured rate 
13:330:25195:28:28:72:509:652:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in area type (central city, rest of MSA, non-MSA, cell phone) a within state s. Further, ߠ௔௧ 
13:331:25287:28:28:72:538:624:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:denotes time fixed effects for each month or year * area type combination; these control for time 
13:332:25385:28:27:72:543:596:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:as flexibly as possible and also allow time trends to evolve differentially across individuals living 
13:333:25487:27:29:72:543:569:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in the four different constructed area types. Finally, ߙ௔௦ denotes fixed effects for each geographic 
13:334:25588:29:28:72:514:540:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:area (e.g., central city in Georgia), and ߝ௜௔௦௧ is a standard error term. Note that ܱܶܵܲ௧ is not 
13:335:25685:28:28:72:532:512:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:separately included in equation (1) since it is absorbed by the time fixed effects (ߠ௔௧), while the 
13:336:25785:28:28:72:530:484:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:terms ܦܧܴܷܵܰܫܷܰ௔௦ܦܫܣܥܫܦܧܯ∗௦ are not separately included in equation (1) since they are 
13:337:25872:28:28:72:271:456:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:absorbed by the area fixed effects ߙሺ௔௦).  
13:338:25915:28:28:107:528:428:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The effect of the ACA on health behaviors without the Medicaid expansion is given by 
13:339:26000:28:30:71:511:400:144:g_d0_f6:144:g_d0_f4:ߛଵ	*ܦܧܴܷܵܰܫܷܰ௔௦, which means it is assumed to be zero in a (hypothetical) area with a 0 
13:340:26088:30:28:72:527:370:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:percent uninsured rate at baseline and increases linearly as the pre-reform uninsured rate rises. 
13:341:26186:28:28:72:531:342:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The identifying assumption is that, in the absence of the ACA, any changes in health behaviors 
13:342:26281:28:27:72:530:314:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that would have occurred in 2014–2016 would not have varied differentially by area uninsured 
13:343:26374:27:28:72:511:287:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:rates, conditional on the controls. We do not need to assume that there would have been no 
13:344:26465:28:27:72:519:259:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:changes at all in health behaviors in the absence of the ACA (conditional on the controls), as 
13:345:26560:27:28:72:527:232:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:would be the case in a pre-post comparison that did not employ variation in pre-reform (2013) 
13:346:26654:28:28:72:154:204:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:uninsured rates.  
13:347:26672:28:28:108:492:176:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f6:The effect of the Medicaid expansion alone on health behaviors is given by ߛଷ∗
13:348:26750:28:28:72:541:148:144:g_d0_f6:144:g_d0_f4:ܦܧܴܷܵܰܫܷܰ௔௦ܦܫܣܥܫܦܧܯ∗௦. As with the other pieces of the ACA, this approach assumes that 
13:349:26837:28:28:72:492:120:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the impact of the Medicaid expansion varies linearly with the state’s pre-reform (2013) 
13:350:26925:28:0:71:539:92:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:uninsured rate. Following Miller (2012a) and Courtemanche et al. (2017, 2018a), we consider ߛଶ 
14:351:27023:0:27:72:533:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to represent unobserved confounders rather than capturing part of the expansion’s causal effect, 
14:352:27120:27:28:72:519:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:since the Medicaid expansion should not causally affect coverage in an area with a 0 percent 
14:353:27213:28:27:72:535:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:baseline uninsured rate. The identifying assumption for the impact of the Medicaid expansion is 
14:354:27309:27:28:72:537:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:therefore that, in the absence of the ACA, differential changes in health behaviors in 2014–2016 
14:355:27406:28:28:72:532:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:between Medicaid expansion and nonexpansion states would not have been correlated with pre-
14:356:27497:28:27:72:530:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reform uninsured rates. This is of course a weaker assumption than would be required by a DD 
14:357:27590:27:28:72:443:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:model examining the impact of the Medicaid expansion on health behaviors. 
14:358:27664:28:27:108:533:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:We also estimate several variants of equation (1) as robustness checks. First, since some 
14:359:27754:27:28:72:527:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:control variables (e.g. income, education, marital status) could be endogenous to the ACA, we 
14:360:27848:28:28:72:505:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:re-estimate our main regression including only the demographic controls age, gender, and 
14:361:27937:28:27:72:535:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:race/ethnicity. The second and third checks involve two alternate ways to compute pretreatment 
14:362:28032:27:28:72:540:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:uninsured rates: 1) pooling all three pretreatment years rather than just using 2013, and 2) simply 
14:363:28132:28:27:72:537:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:using state-level estimates rather than defining sub-state areas based on the “area type” variable. 
14:364:28232:27:28:72:517:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:For the fourth check, we drop respondents interviewed on cell phones since we do not know 
14:365:28322:28:27:72:542:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:whether they live in a central city, suburban area, or rural area. Recall that we originally included 
14:366:28424:27:28:72:503:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:them as a distinct sub-state group. Next, we drop 19-25 year olds since they were already 
14:367:28514:28:28:72:531:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:partially treated by the ACA dependent coverage expansion that took effect in 2010. In another 
14:368:28609:28:27:72:522:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:check, we drop the five “early expansion” states that Kaestner et al. (2017) classify as having 
14:369:28705:27:28:72:518:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:comprehensive Medicaid expansions under the ACA before 2014. Lastly, we drop the seven 
14:370:28792:28:22:72:514:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:states who took up the Medicaid expansion after January 2014 (Courtemanche et al., 2017). 
14:371:28882:22:19:72:74:162:36:g_d0_f3:36:g_d0_f3: 
14:372:28882:19:28:72:112:143:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:Results 
14:373:28890:28:27:108:521:115:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:  Tables 2 and 3 report the results from the baseline DDD regression for the preventive 
14:374:28978:27:0:72:510:88:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:care and risky behavior outcomes, respectively. The results from the robustness checks are 
15:375:29073:0:27:72:523:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:generally quite similar; they are available in Tables A2-A11 of the Online Appendix. The top 
15:376:29166:27:28:72:515:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:panel of each table presents the coefficient estimates and standard errors for the variables of 
15:377:29262:28:27:72:508:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:interest, while the bottom panel gives the implied effects of the ACA with and without the 
15:378:29353:27:28:72:534:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Medicaid expansion at the average pretreatment uninsured rate of 0.206. Indicators of statistical 
15:379:29451:28:28:72:536:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:significance are given at the 0.1 percent, 1 percent, and 5 percent level. Table A12 of the Online 
15:380:29550:28:27:72:502:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Appendix reports all of the regression coefficients from our baseline regressions for each 
15:381:29641:27:28:72:119:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:outcome. 
15:382:29650:28:27:108:529:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The results from Table 2 show that the fully implemented ACA increased utilization of 
15:383:29736:27:28:72:540:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:all the preventive care outcomes in non-Medicaid-expansion states, with the effects on checkups, 
15:384:29833:28:28:72:500:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:pap tests, and HIV tests being statistically significant at the 5 percent level or better. The 
15:385:29928:28:27:72:519:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:magnitudes of the increases are 3.8, 1.9, 4.3, 1.5, and 2.1 percentage points for checkups, flu 
15:386:30024:27:28:72:484:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:shots, pap tests, mammograms, and HIV tests, respectively. To provide a benchmark, 
15:387:30107:28:27:72:538:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Courtemanche et al. (2018b) estimated that the full ACA increased health insurance coverage by 
15:388:30202:27:28:72:537:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:9.5 percentage points using the same BRFSS data and econometric model. If we assume that the 
15:389:30295:28:27:72:524:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ACA only affected preventive care via the extensive margin of health insurance coverage, the 
15:390:30388:27:28:72:524:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:results therefore imply that between 17 and 50 percent of newly insured individuals increased 
15:391:30482:28:28:72:541:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:their preventive care usage, depending on the outcome. Moreover, the estimated effects represent 
15:392:30579:28:27:72:522:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:between 5 and 11 percent of the outcomes’ sample means. For these reasons, we consider the 
15:393:30670:27:28:72:493:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:magnitude of the increase in preventive care utilization to be economically meaningful. 
15:394:30758:28:27:72:523:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Interestingly, all the gains appear to be attributable to the private expansion component of the 
15:395:30855:27:28:72:540:157:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ACA (the package of reforms that took effect nationally), as opposed to the Medicaid expansion. 
15:396:30951:28:0:72:527:129:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The effects of the Medicaid expansion are statistically insignificant and relatively small for all 
16:397:31054:0:27:72:518:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:outcomes. Moreover, the increase in mammograms actually becomes significant without the 
16:398:31142:27:28:72:181:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Medicaid expansion.   
16:399:31164:28:27:108:489:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Turning to the risky health behavior outcomes in Table 3, we observe only one 
16:400:31242:27:28:71:532:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:statistically significant effect of the full ACA: a 1.6 percentage point increase in the probability 
16:401:31343:28:28:71:521:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of being a risky drinker. This represents a sizeable 7.4 percent increase relative to the sample 
16:402:31440:28:27:71:541:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:mean. Again, the effect is nearly completely driven by the private portion of the ACA rather than 
16:403:31538:27:28:71:527:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the Medicaid expansion. The point estimates for drinks per month, smoking, and exercise also 
16:404:31631:28:27:71:535:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:point in the direction of worse health behaviors, while the reverse is true for BMI. However, the 
16:405:31729:27:28:71:519:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:effects for these outcomes are all insignificant and much smaller in magnitude relative to the 
16:406:31824:28:22:71:149:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sample mean.    
16:407:31840:22:19:75:77:438:144:g_d0_f4:36:g_d0_f4:  
16:408:31840:19:28:72:174:419:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:Event Study Model 
16:409:31858:28:28:108:512:391:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:We next estimate an event study model to trace out the ACA’s impact by year. This 
16:410:31940:28:27:71:483:363:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:serves two purposes. First, such an approach provides indirect tests of the identifying 
16:411:32028:27:28:71:480:336:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:assumptions of our DDD model, as any “effects” of the ACA that emerge during the 
16:412:32109:28:27:72:541:308:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:pretreatment period likely reflect placebo test failures. Second, the event study model also allows 
16:413:32209:27:28:72:523:281:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:us to distinguish between the ACA’s effects in each of the three post-treatment years, thereby 
16:414:32304:28:27:72:528:253:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:specifically illustrating the importance of our addition of 2016 to the sample period. The event 
16:415:32401:27:28:72:454:226:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:study model is given by the following equation, with 2013 being the base year:

16:422:32838:14:14:255:546:113:144:g_d0_f6:144:g_d0_f4:2015ܻ௧ߠሻ൅ଵହܦܧܴܷܵܰܫܷܰሺ௔௦ܦܫܣܥܫܦܧܯ∗௦∗2016ܻ௧ሻ  
16:423:32881:14:0:288:542:99:144:g_d0_f6:144:g_d0_f4:൅ࣂ૚૟ࢄ࢚࢙ࢇ࢏ߙ൅௔௦ߝ൅௜௔௦௧                                       (2) 
17:424:32947:0:27:72:515:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:where Y2011t, Y2012t, Y2014t, Y2015t, and Y2016t are indicators for each year and the other 
17:425:33039:27:28:71:504:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:variables are defined as in equation (1). The tests for differential pretreatment trends (i.e., 
17:426:33135:28:27:72:502:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:falsification tests) are provided by evaluating whether the coefficients on the “treatment” 
17:427:33227:27:28:72:391:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:variables in the pretreatment years (θ1, θ2, θ11, θ12) are equal to 0.8 
17:428:33298:28:28:108:519:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Table 4 presents the event study results for the preventive care outcomes and Table 5 
17:429:33384:28:27:72:525:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:does the same for the risky health behavior outcomes. Note that the pap test and mammogram 
17:430:33475:27:28:72:531:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:variables are not available in 2013 and 2015, so 2012 is the base year for those outcomes while 
17:431:33571:28:27:72:498:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:no effect is reported in 2015. There are a total of 36 falsification tests (four pretreatment 
17:432:33665:27:28:72:529:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:interaction terms for each of the eight outcomes with no missing years plus two for each of the 
17:433:33761:28:28:72:530:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:two remaining outcomes). We obtain four falsification test failures, or eleven percent, which is 
17:434:33858:28:27:72:531:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reasonably close to the five percent that would expected due to chance, albeit somewhat larger. 
17:435:33954:27:28:72:523:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Two of the four failures are for the checkup variable, meaning that for the other outcomes the 
17:436:34049:28:27:72:534:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:falsification tests only fail six percent of the time. One way to interpret these results is therefore 
17:437:34152:27:28:72:529:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that the estimated increase in well-patient checkups should be treated with caution, but that the 
17:438:34250:28:27:72:345:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:DDD model performs quite well for the other outcomes. 
17:439:34304:27:28:108:524:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The coefficient estimates on the treatment variables in the post-reform years provide a 
17:440:34392:28:28:72:495:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:few new insights. First, new evidence of ex ante moral hazard emerges in the third post-
17:441:34480:28:27:72:514:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:treatment year: a statistically significant increase in smoking (1.8 percentage points) among 
17:442:34574:27:28:72:515:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:those in non-expansion states in 2016 and a statistically significant decrease in exercise (2.7 
17:443:34670:28:27:72:528:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:percentage points) in Medicaid expansion states. These findings were masked in the aggregate 
17:444:34763:27:36:72:541:157:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:post-reform results reported in Table 3, and were also not found by either of the prior studies that 
17:445:34864:36:8:72:218:121:121:g_d0_f2:121:g_d0_f2:                                                            
17:446:34924:8:7:72:258:113:42:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f6:8 Recall that the coefficient on the ܦܫܣܥܫܦܧܯ
17:447:34969:7:9:258:525:106:49:g_d0_f6:100:g_d0_f4:௦ܱܶܵܲ∗௧ variable in our main regression was assumed to capture 
17:448:35032:9:12:71:508:97:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:unobserved confounders rather than part of the causal effect of the Medicaid expansion. We therefore do not 
17:449:35140:12:11:71:537:85:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:consider θ6 and θ7 to provide additional falsification tests and do not show the estimates for these coefficients in the 
17:450:35261:11:0:72:100:74:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:tables. 
18:451:35273:0:27:72:497:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:used only two post-treatment years. Second, we observe some evidence that the gains in 
18:452:35360:27:28:72:478:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:preventive care diminish over time. The full ACA increased flu shots, pap tests, and 
18:453:35445:28:27:72:540:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:mammograms by 3.8, 6.2, and 2.5 percentage points, respectively, in the first year after the ACA 
18:454:35542:27:28:72:499:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:took effect, and all of these effects are statistically significant. In contrast, by 2016 these 
18:455:35638:28:28:72:511:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:magnitudes had fallen to 2.8, 3.1, and 1.8 percentage points, none of which are statistically 
18:456:35732:28:27:72:536:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:significant. If taken at face value, these results are consistent with pent-up demand driving some 
18:457:35831:27:28:72:497:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of the increase in 2014. However, the estimates from 2014 and 2016 are not statistically 
18:458:35920:28:27:72:535:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:different, so we are reluctant to emphasize such an interpretation. Moreover, the results for HIV 
18:459:36018:27:28:72:528:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:tests do not fit this pattern, as the effects (at least in non-expansion states) are stronger in 2016 
18:460:36120:28:22:72:188:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and 2015 than in 2014.  
18:461:36144:22:19:72:74:438:36:g_d0_f4:36:g_d0_f4: 
18:462:36144:19:28:72:179:419:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:Subsample Analyses 
18:463:36163:28:28:108:518:391:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:We next conduct subsample analyses that stratify the sample by income and age. We 
18:464:36245:28:27:71:525:363:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expect the ACA’s impacts to be most pronounced among young adults and individuals of low 
18:465:36334:27:28:71:543:336:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:socio-economic-status, as these groups experienced the largest insurance coverage gains from the 
18:466:36431:28:27:71:483:308:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ACA (Courtemanche et al., 2018c). Specifically, we re-estimate equation (1) for four 
18:467:36516:27:28:71:534:281:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:subsamples: those with household incomes above and below the sample median, and those with 
18:468:36607:28:27:71:506:253:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:age above and below the sample median. For each subsample, we re-compute the relevant 
18:469:36693:27:28:71:541:226:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:pretreatment uninsured rate based on only the respondents in that particular subsample. The need 
18:470:36790:28:28:72:514:198:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to retain a sufficient number of individuals in each local area in each subsample to compute 
18:471:36883:28:27:72:542:170:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reliable pretreatment uninsured rates is why we only stratify into two broad categories along each 
18:472:36982:27:28:72:131:143:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:dimension.  
18:473:36994:28:27:108:540:115:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f4: Table 6 reports the results for the low income subsample while Table 7 does the same for 
18:474:37083:27:0:72:499:88:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the high income subsample. For the preventive care outcomes, the effects appear largely 
19:475:37175:0:27:72:539:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:concentrated among those with incomes below the median. The impacts of the private portion of 
19:476:37269:27:28:72:511:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the ACA on checkups, mammograms, and HIV tests are statistically significant for the low 
19:477:37358:28:27:72:520:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:income subsample, and the magnitudes are larger than those from the full sample reported in 
19:478:37450:27:28:72:524:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Table 2. In contrast, we observe statistically insignificant and relatively small effects on these 
19:479:37549:28:28:72:541:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:outcomes among those above the median income, and the signs are mixed. The only exception to 
19:480:37642:28:27:72:533:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:this pattern is that the magnitude of the effect of the full ACA on pap tests is almost identical in 
19:481:37743:27:28:72:535:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the two subsamples, and is only significant among those with higher incomes since the standard 
19:482:37838:28:27:72:513:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:error is much smaller for that group. Turning to the risky behavior outcomes, ex ante moral 
19:483:37930:27:28:71:541:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:hazard appears more prevalent among the high income subsample. As shown in the bottom panel 
19:484:38022:28:28:71:525:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of Table 7, for this subsample the estimated effects of the full ACA are in the direction of less 
19:485:38120:28:27:71:539:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:healthy behaviors for all outcomes. The only result that is statistically significant at the 5 percent 
19:486:38223:27:28:71:536:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:level or better is the estimated increase in smoking, while the rise in risky drinking is significant 
19:487:38325:28:27:71:520:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:at the 10 percent level. Though statistically insignificant, the estimated increase in drinks per 
19:488:38423:27:28:72:525:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:month is economically meaningful at 3.3 percent of the sample mean. Among the low income 
19:489:38512:28:27:72:533:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:subsample, the pattern of signs is more mixed, with the full ACA leading to healthier behaviors 
19:490:38608:27:28:72:487:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:for three outcomes and less healthy behaviors for two, and none of these estimates are 
19:491:38695:28:28:72:281:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:statistically significant or especially large.9 
19:492:38742:28:27:107:535:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Tables 8 and 9 report the results for the age subsamples. For preventive care, we see that 
19:493:38833:27:28:71:505:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the estimated effects of the full ACA are consistently positive across all outcomes in both 
19:494:38925:28:29:71:536:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:subsamples. While the impact on checkups is an identical 4.1 percentage points for both groups, 
19:495:39021:29:8:72:218:155:121:g_d0_f2:121:g_d0_f2:                                                            
19:496:39081:8:16:72:518:147:42:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:9 The BRFSS only reports income in ranges (e.g. $15,000 to $19,999), so we assign each respondent a value of 
19:497:39190:16:11:72:491:131:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:income equal to the midpoint of the associated category when doing the stratification. Since this leads to 
19:498:39297:11:12:72:515:120:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:measurement error, we also tried assessing heterogeneity in impacts by socioeconomic status by stratifying by 
19:499:39407:12:11:72:542:108:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:education level.  These results are available upon request.  Because the differences across subsamples were less clear 
19:500:39526:11:12:72:537:97:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:stratifying by education than income, we decided to report the income subsamples only in the body of this paper. In 
19:501:39642:12:11:72:517:85:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:other words, our results suggest that BRFSS income, despite its measurement error, is a better proxy for actual 
19:502:39754:11:0:71:164:74:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:income that education. 
20:503:39781:0:27:72:541:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the increases in pap and HIV tests are only statistically significant in the younger subsample, and 
20:504:39881:27:28:72:505:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:they are much larger than the corresponding increases in the older subsample (4.9 and 3.8 
20:505:39971:28:27:72:483:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:percentage points compared to 2.0 and 0.1 percentage points). In contrast, the gain in 
20:506:40058:27:28:72:516:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:mammograms is concentrated among the older subsample, consistent with them being more 
20:507:40144:28:28:72:538:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:commonly recommended for that age group. For risky behaviors, the patterns are mixed, and the 
20:508:40238:28:27:72:541:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:only estimates significant at the 5 percent level are an increase in drinks per month from the fully 
20:509:40339:27:28:71:522:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:implemented ACA for the older subsample and an increase in risky drinking from the private 
20:510:40430:28:27:71:313:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:portion of the ACA for the younger subsample.     
20:511:40480:27:28:107:524:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:In an additional set of regressions, we also examined the impacts of the ACA on those 
20:512:40566:28:28:71:519:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:aged 65 and above as a falsification test. This is because those aged 65 and above should not 
20:513:40660:28:27:71:541:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:have been directly impacted by the ACA, as they were already covered by Medicare. The results, 
20:514:40755:27:28:71:498:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reported in Table 10, were generally both statistically and economically insignificant, as 
20:515:40846:28:22:71:126:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expected.   
20:516:40858:22:19:108:110:355:36:g_d0_f3:36:g_d0_f3: 
20:517:40858:19:28:72:372:336:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:Difference-in-Difference Analysis for Medicaid Expansion 
20:518:40915:28:27:108:515:308:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:In the above DDD analyses, we repeatedly find little to no evidence of effects of the 
20:519:41001:27:28:71:539:281:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Medicaid expansion on the various outcomes. While this could indicate genuine null effects, it is 
20:520:41099:28:27:71:506:253:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:also possible that the DDD model is poorly suited to identify plausibly sized effects of the 
20:521:41192:27:28:71:542:226:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Medicaid expansion. The DDD estimator for the triple interaction term is inherently less efficient 
20:522:41291:28:28:71:514:198:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:than the simpler DD estimator used by most evaluations of the ACA’s Medicaid expansion. 
20:523:41379:28:27:71:528:170:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Additionally, as discussed in the previous section, it is impractical to estimate our DDD model 
20:524:41475:27:28:71:488:143:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:with narrowly defined subsamples because of the need to retain a sufficient number of 
20:525:41561:28:0:71:525:115:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:individuals to compute pretreatment uninsured rates in each local area for each subsample. As 
21:526:41659:0:27:72:520:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:such, it may be difficult to identify the effects of a narrowly targeted intervention such as the 
21:527:41757:27:28:72:453:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Medicaid expansion, which primarily focused on low-income childless adults.  
21:528:41834:28:27:108:522:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:For these reasons, we close our empirical analysis by estimating a DD model with the 
21:529:41919:27:28:72:517:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sample restricted to adults with household income below 100 percent of the Federal Poverty 
21:530:42010:28:28:72:520:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Level (FPL) and no children in the home. The first restriction prevents confounding from the 
21:531:42103:28:27:72:528:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ACA’s expansion of private coverage, since those with incomes below 100 percent of the FPL 
21:532:42194:27:28:72:492:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:were prohibited from purchasing subsidized insurance on the Marketplace. The second 
21:533:42278:28:27:72:524:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:restriction minimizes the share of the sample that was eligible for Medicaid prior to the ACA, 
21:534:42373:27:28:72:489:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:since eligibility criteria were more generous for adults with children in most states. By 
21:535:42463:28:28:72:523:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:estimating a DD model with BRFSS data and a low-income subsample, our approach mirrors 
21:536:42550:28:27:72:539:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that of Simon et al. (2017), with the key difference being our inclusion of the third posttreatment 
21:537:42650:27:29:71:514:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f6:year. Specifically, the DD model is identical to equation (1) but drops the ܦܧܴܷܵܰܫܷܰ௔௦∗
21:538:42738:29:28:71:516:376:144:g_d0_f6:144:g_d0_f6:ܦܫܣܥܫܦܧܯ௦ܱܶܵܲ∗௧ term. Results from regressions that further drop the ܦܧܴܷܵܰܫܷܰ௔௦∗
21:539:42819:28:27:71:392:348:144:g_d0_f6:144:g_d0_f4:ܱܶܵܲ௧ term are virtually identical and are available upon request.  
21:540:42886:27:28:108:535:321:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Table 11 reports the results. Even in the less rigorous DD model with a sample narrowly 
21:541:42974:28:28:72:512:293:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:restricted to the individuals most likely to benefit from the Medicaid expansion, we find no 
21:542:43067:28:27:72:539:265:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:statistically significant effects on any of the ten outcomes in either direction. The magnitudes are 
21:543:43168:27:28:72:535:238:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:all relatively small, at least compared to the 9.8 percentage point increase in insurance coverage 
21:544:43267:28:28:72:521:210:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:estimated using the same sample and DD method. Additionally, the signs are mixed, with six 
21:545:43358:28:27:72:533:182:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:pointing in a favorable direction (greater preventive care use, healthier lifestyle habits) and four 
21:546:43459:27:28:72:515:155:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in an unfavorable direction. Therefore, the null results for the Medicaid expansion observed 
21:547:43552:28:27:72:495:127:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:throughout the paper do not appear to simply be due to limitations of the DDD design.   
21:548:43640:27:0:72:75:100:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3: 
22:549:43644:0:27:72:129:708:144:g_d0_f3:144:g_d0_f3:Discussion 
22:550:43655:27:28:72:538:681:121:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f4: This paper estimates the effects of the ACA on the health behaviors of non-elderly adults 
22:551:43746:28:27:72:535:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:after three years. We consider two distinct types of outcomes: positive health investments in the 
22:552:43844:27:28:72:517:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:form of preventive services and disinvestments in the form of risky behaviors. Both types of 
22:553:43937:28:28:72:530:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:behaviors could theoretically be influenced by both the reduction in effective prices of medical 
22:554:44034:28:27:71:520:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:services after obtaining insurance coverage and ex ante moral hazard from the expectation of 
22:555:44127:27:28:71:506:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:lower out-of-pocket costs in the future if a preventable illness occurs. Using data from the 
22:556:44220:28:27:71:524:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:BRFSS and an identification strategy that leverages variation in pre-ACA uninsured rates and 
22:557:44313:27:28:71:536:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:state Medicaid expansion decisions, we find some evidence to support both of these hypotheses. 
22:558:44408:28:28:71:533:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The ACA increased preventive care along several dimensions, including well-patient checkups, 
22:559:44501:28:27:71:540:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:pap tests, mammograms, and HIV tests. This is consistent with the direct price effect dominating 
22:560:44598:27:28:72:500:405:144:Times:144:g_d0_f4:ex ante moral hazard for preventive services, which makes sense since these services are 
22:561:44687:28:27:72:528:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:purchased directly in the medical sector. In contrast, while certain medical treatments can help 
22:562:44784:27:28:72:532:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:individuals make healthier lifestyle choices, the prices of food, gym memberships, alcohol, and 
22:563:44880:28:27:72:539:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:Times:cigarettes are not directly influenced by health insurance. Perhaps for this reason, we find that ex 
22:564:44981:27:28:71:516:295:144:Times:144:g_d0_f4:ante moral hazard dominates for at least some risky behaviors, while other behaviors do not 
22:565:45073:28:28:71:340:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:seem to be influenced by insurance in either direction.   
22:566:45131:28:27:107:533:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: An interesting aspect of our results is that we consistently find that any observed effects 
22:567:45223:27:28:71:533:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:are attributable to the “private portion” of the ACA – the package of national reforms including 
22:568:45320:28:27:72:505:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:regulations in the non-group insurance market, mandates, and subsidized health insurance 
22:569:45409:27:28:72:541:157:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:exchanges – as opposed to the Medicaid expansion. This echoes the findings of Courtemanche et 
22:570:45503:28:28:72:526:129:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:al. (2018a, 2018b) that improvements in access to care and self-assessed health from the ACA 
22:571:45596:28:0:72:486:101:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:are driven mostly by the private expansion. One possible explanation is that Medicaid 
23:572:45686:0:27:72:537:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:historically pays providers much less than private insurers, which can lead to difficulty finding a 
23:573:45786:27:28:72:540:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:primary care doctor or specialist.10 Since the medical services under consideration in this paper – 
23:574:45886:28:27:72:539:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:screenings, vaccines, counseling services, and prescriptions for weight loss or smoking cessation 
23:575:45984:27:28:72:524:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:drugs – are generally administered in office-based settings, this could potentially help explain 
23:576:46081:28:28:72:192:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Medicaid’s null effects.  
23:577:46107:28:27:108:525:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:However, other factors argue against this explanation. Medicaid increased its payment 
23:578:46193:27:28:72:526:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:rates to match those of Medicare in 2013 and 2014, with only a few states maintaining the full 
23:579:46288:28:27:72:489:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:fee bump thereafter.11 Polsky et al. (2015) show that the Medicaid fee bump improved 
23:580:46373:27:28:72:524:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:availability of primary care appointments for enrollees. This implies that, if provider payment 
23:581:46469:28:28:72:492:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:rates are the issue, we should have found a positive of effect of Medicaid expansion on 
23:582:46557:28:27:72:538:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:preventive care in 2014 followed by a reduction in 2015, rather than null effects across all years. 
23:583:46657:27:28:72:518:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Moreover, results from the randomized Oregon Medicaid experiment indicate that Medicaid 
23:584:46745:28:27:72:541:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:increased use of preventive services, even without the fee bump (Finkelstein et al., 2012; Baicker 
23:585:46844:27:28:71:541:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:et al., 2013). In sum, it is difficult to say more at this stage beyond simply that Medicaid’s effects 
23:586:46947:28:27:71:522:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:may vary by context, which could include both demand-side factors such as the demographic 
23:587:47037:27:28:71:534:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:characteristics of newly covered individuals or supply-side factors such as supply of health care 
23:588:47135:28:28:71:541:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:providers. Additionally, a Medicaid expansion could conceivably be less effective when it occurs 
23:589:47232:28:27:71:515:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:alongside an expansion of private coverage (i.e. Marketplace coverage), as the expansion of 
23:590:47324:27:28:71:375:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:private coverage places additional strain on provider capacity.  
23:591:47389:28:27:107:541:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Another noteworthy result is that, while the majority of our results for risky behaviors are 
23:592:47482:27:28:71:503:157:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:statistically insignificant, we find relatively robust evidence that the ACA increased risky 
23:593:47575:28:20:71:518:129:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:drinking. It is possible that a single result could emerge by chance given the large number of 
23:594:47670:20:8:72:218:109:121:g_d0_f2:121:g_d0_f2:                                                            
23:595:47730:8:16:72:502:101:42:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:10 See, for instance, https://health.usnews.com/health-news/health-insurance/articles/2015/05/26/youve-got-
23:596:47837:16:7:72:235:85:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:medicaid-why-cant-you-see-the-doctor.  
23:597:47876:7:0:72:474:78:42:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:11 See https://www.advisory.com/daily-briefing/2015/04/23/states-to-continue-medicaid-pay-bump.  
24:598:47977:0:27:72:503:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:hypotheses tested in our paper,12 but it is also possible that ex ante moral hazard could be 
24:599:48070:27:28:72:538:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:especially pronounced in the case of risky drinking. One argument for why ex ante moral hazard 
24:600:48165:28:27:71:531:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:has not been found more frequently in the literature is that diseases from many risky behaviors, 
24:601:48262:27:28:71:493:626:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:such as cancer or heart disease from smoking or obesity, often occur far into the future. 
24:602:48352:28:28:71:530:598:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Individuals may assume that even if they are currently uninsured, they will be insured (perhaps 
24:603:48448:28:27:71:532:570:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:by Medicare or a future job) by time those illnesses occur (Barbaresco et al., 2015). In contrast, 
24:604:48547:27:28:71:513:543:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:excessive drinking can lead to the need for expensive medical care immediately, such as an 
24:605:48638:28:27:71:398:515:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ambulance ride and hospitalization due to acute alcohol poisoning.  
24:606:48706:27:28:107:536:488:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Our work is subject to several limitations, including general concerns about the extent to 
24:607:48797:28:28:71:506:460:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:which the self-reported outcomes we examine accurately measure the behavior of BRFSS 
24:608:48882:28:27:71:521:432:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:respondents. That being said, subjective self-reported health variables have been shown to be 
24:609:48976:27:28:71:511:405:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:correlated with objective measures of health, such as mortality (Idler and Benyamini 1997; 
24:610:49067:28:27:71:511:377:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:DeSalvo et al. 2006; Phillips, Der, and Carroll 2010). In our particular context, one may be 
24:611:49160:27:28:71:531:350:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:additionally concerned that gains in coverage led to gains in knowledge about health status and 
24:612:49256:28:27:71:538:322:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:behaviors through increased interaction with health care providers and that this led to changes in 
24:613:49355:27:28:71:306:295:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reporting rather than actual changes in behavior. 
24:614:49405:28:28:107:529:267:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: In closing, while our research offers important new information about the effects of the 
24:615:49494:28:27:71:533:239:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ACA over a longer time frame than most prior studies, our results nonetheless provide only one 
24:616:49589:27:28:71:524:212:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:piece of a much larger puzzle. Any comprehensive evaluation of the ACA would have to take 
24:617:49679:28:29:71:506:184:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:into account effects on a wide range of other outcomes, including overall health, financial 
24:618:49771:29:8:72:218:155:121:g_d0_f2:121:g_d0_f2:                                                            
24:619:49831:8:16:72:538:147:42:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:12 Following arguments made in other studies that looked at a similar number of outcomes, such as Barbaresco et al. 
24:620:49947:16:11:72:539:131:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:(2015), we do not implement formal methods for multiple hypothesis testing because, while these control the Type I 
24:621:50062:11:12:72:509:120:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:error rate, they do so at the expense of a large increase in the Type II error rate. While we cannot rule out the 
24:622:50176:12:11:72:533:108:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:possibility of an occasional spurious result given the number of hypotheses tested, it is worth noting that we obtain 
24:623:50294:11:12:72:526:97:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:many more statistically significant results than would be expected due to chance. For instance, in Tables 2 and 3, 
24:624:50409:12:11:72:539:85:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:which contain our baseline results, we report twenty coefficient estimates. Five are significant at the 5 percent level, 
24:625:50530:11:0:72:336:74:100:g_d0_f4:100:g_d0_f4:whereas only one would be expected to be significant by chance.  
25:626:50599:0:27:72:534:708:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:protection, health care expenditures, fiscal costs, employment, and wages. We contribute to this 
25:627:50696:27:28:72:512:681:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:broader debate by providing new evidence that the ACA increased utilization of preventive 
25:628:50786:28:22:72:432:653:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:services but led to less healthy lifestyles along at least some dimensions.
